The manuscript by Sydenham quoted on p. 10 is from Kenneth Dewhurst, [3mJohn Locke, Physician and Philosopher: A Medical Biography [0m
(Wellcome Historical Medical Library, 1963). Henry Iretonโs question to the Leveller leaders in the Putney debates quoted on p. 43 
is taken from A. S. P. Woodhouse (ed.), [3mPuritanism and Liberty [0m(J. M. Dent & Son, 1938). Coleridgeโs comment cited on p. 97 comes 
from Kathleen Coburn (ed.), [3mThe Notebooks of Samuel Taylor Coleridge, [0mii. 1804โ1808 (New York, 1961), entry 2627. 

[1mูุฑุงุกุงุช ุฅุถุงููุฉ [0m

Full bibliographical details of the editions of Lockeโs works that have been used in references are given in the list of 
abbreviations at the beginning of the book. 

The Clarendon Press is at present engaged in publishing an edition of all Lockeโs published writings and many of his unpublished 
manuscripts. The [3mEssay concerning Human Understanding [0mand (thus far) eight volumes of his [3mCorrespondence [0mwere the first to appear, 
superlatively edited by Peter Nidditch and E. S. de Beer respectively. A single volume of [3mSelected Correspondence [0m, edited by Mark 
Goldie (Oxford University Press), now makes some of the vividness and fascination of the full [3mCorrespondence [0maccessible to a wider 
readership. A final volume, along with a full index to the [3mCorrespondence [0mas a whole, will be issued shortly. These two works have 
since been joined by A [3mParaphrase and Notes on the Epistles of St Paul [0m(ed. Arthur A. Wainwright, 2 vols., 1987), [3mSome Thoughts on 
Education [0m(ed. John W. & Jean S. Yolton, 1989), [3mDrafts for the Essay concerning Human Understanding and other Philosophical 
Writings [0m(ed. Peter H. Nidditch and G. A. J. Rogers, 1990), [3mLocke on Money [0m(2 vols., ed. Patrick Hyde Kelly, 1991), and [3mThe 
Reasonableness of Christianity [0m(ed. John Higgins-Biddle, 1999). There are also excellent modern editions of the Two [3mTreatises of 
Government [0m(ed. Peter Laslett, Cambridge University Press, 2nd edn., 1988), [3mTwo Tracts on Government [0m(ed. Philip Abrams, Cambridge 
University Press, 1968), [3mEssays on the Law of Nature [0m(ed. W. von Leyden, Clarendon Press, 1954), and a somewhat less satisfactory 
edition of the [3mLetter on Toleration [0m(ed. R. Klibansky and J. W. Gough, Clarendon Press, 1967). There are also very useful 
selections across the range of Lockeโs views about politics in David Woottonโs [3mPolitical Writings of John Locke [0m(Penguin, 1993), a 
full and careful presentation of many of his incidental writings on politics in Locke, [3mPolitical Essays, [0med. Mark Goldie 
(Cambridge University Press, 1997), and a valuable selection of Lockeโs [3mWritings on Religion [0m(ed. Victor Nuovo, Clarendon Press, 
2002). Other published works of Locke are still most conveniently consulted in the 18th โ or 19th โ century editions of his [3m
Collected Works. [0m

Maurice Cranstonโs [3mJohn Locke: A Biography [0m(Longman, London, 1957) is informative but less vivid than Laslettโs Introduction to 
the [3mTwo Treatises [0m. There is a major modern biography of Shaftesbury by K. H. D. Haley, [3mThe First Earl of Shaftesbury [0m(Clarendon 
Press, Oxford, 1968), and a remarkable (if not invariably reliable) study of Lockeโs role in Shaftesburyโs political enterprises 
in the late Richard Ashcraftโs [3mRevolutionary Politics [0mand [3mLockeโs Two Treatises of Government [0m(Princeton University Press, 1986). 
The Introductions by von Leyden and Abrams are particularly illuminating on the development of Lockeโs understanding of morality. 
The best systematic treatments of this are now provided by John Colman, [3mJohn Lockeโs Moral Philosophy [0m(Edinburgh University Press, 
1983) and A. John Simmons, [3mThe Lockean Theory of Rights [0m(Princeton University Press, 1992); but see also, more broadly, Ian 
Harris, [3mThe Mind of John Locke [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1994). Lockeโs religious views are clearly (and on the whole 
approvingly) presented in M. S. Johnson, [3mLocke on Freedom [0m(Best Printing Co., Austin, Texas, 1978). They are also now widely 
discussed in studies of his political thinking (see, e.g., Dunn, 1969; Tully, 1980 and 1993; Marshall, 1994; Harris, 1994 below). 

Michael Ayersโs superb two-volume study [3mLocke. Epistemology and Ontology [0m(Routledge, 1991) stands head and shoulders above all 
other modern philosophical treatments of his philosophy as a whole. Amongst other helpful works, written from a wide variety of 
perspectives, are John W. Yolton, [3mLocke and the Compass of Human Understanding [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1970); Roger 
Woolhouse, [3mLockeโs Philosophy of Science and Knowledge [0m(Basil Blackwell, Oxford, 1971); Richard I. Aaron, [3mJohn Locke, [0m3rd edn. 
(Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1971); James Gibson, [3mLockeโs Theory of Knowledge and its Historical Relations [0m(Cambridge University 
Press, 1917); Kathleen Squadrito, [3mLockeโs Theory of Sensitive Knowledge [0m(University Press of America, Washington, DC, 1978); J. L. 
Mackie, [3mProblems from Locke [0m(Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1976); Jonathan Bennett, [3mLocke, Berkeley, Hume: Central Themes [0m(Clarendon 
Press, Oxford, 1971) and [3mLearning from Six Philosophers [0m, 2 vols. (Oxford University Press, 2001), principally in Vol. 2; Peter A. 
Schouls, [3mThe Imposition of Method [0m(Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1980); and the essays collected in I. C. Tipton (ed.), [3mLocke on Human 
Understanding [0m(Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1977). See now, too, Peter Schouls, [3mReasoned Freedom: John Locke and Enlightenment [0m
(Cornell University Press, 1992) and several of the chapters in Vere Chappell (ed.), [3mThe Cambridge Companion to Locke [0m(Cambridge 
University Press, 1994). There are a number of important articles by Michael Ayers (see particularly โLocke versus Aristotle on 
Natural Kindsโ, [3mJournal of Philosophy, [0mMay 1981; โMechanism, Superaddition and the Proof of Godโs Existence in Lockeโs [3mEssay [0mโ, [3m
Philosophical Review, [0mApril 1981; โThe Ideas of Power and Substance in Lockeโs Philosophyโ, [3mPhilosophical Quarterly, [0mJanuary 
1975). The central importance for Locke of menโs responsibility for their own beliefs is brought out very elegantly in John 
Passmore, โLocke and the Ethics of Beliefโ, [3mProceedings of the British Academy, [0m1978. The relation between his conception of menโs 
natural cognitive powers and the challenges with which History confronts them is discussed in J. Dunn, โโBright Enough for all our 
Purposesโ: John Lockeโs Conception of a Civilised Societyโ, [3mNotes and Records of the Royal Society, [0m43 (1989). On his conception 
of education, see (in addition to the edition of J. W. and J. S. Yolton, 1989) Nathan Tarcov, [3mLockeโs Education for Liberty [0m
(University of Chicago Press, 1984). On Lockeโs conceptions of persons and their identity, see Ruth Mattern, โMoral Science and 
the Concept of Persons in Lockeโ, [3mPhilosophical Review, [0mJanuary 1980, and David Wiggins, โLocke, Butler and the Stream of 
Consciousness and Men as a Natural Kindโ, in A. O. Rorty (ed.), [3mThe Identities of Persons [0m(University of California Press, 
Berkeley, 1976). For the formation of Lockeโs own identity, see J. Dunn, โIndividuality and Clientage in the Formation of Lockeโs 
Social Imaginationโ, in Reinhard Brandt (ed.), [3mJohn Locke [0m(W. de Gruyter, Berlin and New York, 1981). The originality and 
influence of Lockeโs conception of language is discussed magisterially in Hans Aarsleff, [3mFrom Locke to Saussure [0m(Athlone Press, 
London, 1982). 

The best introductions to Lockeโs political thought are Geraint Parry, [3mLocke [0m(George Allen and Unwin, 1978) and Richard Ashcraft, [3m
Lockeโs Two Treatises of Government [0m(George Allen and Unwin, 1987); but compare Ruth W. Grant, [3mJohn Lockeโs Liberalism [0m(University 
of Chicago Press, 1987). The [3mTwo Treatises [0mitself is discussed in J. Dunn, [3mThe Political Thought of John Locke [0m(Cambridge 
University Press, 1969). There are now also extremely valuable overall treatments of the [3mTwo Treatises [0min A. John Simmons, [3mThe 
Lockean Theory of Rights [0mand [3mOn the Edge of Anarchy [0m(Princeton University Press, 1992). Its analysis of property is best treated 
in James Tully, [3mA Discourse of Property [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1980). But compare Tullyโs recent collection, [3mAn Approach to 
Political Philosophy. Locke in Contexts [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1993), Jeremy Waldronโs careful and forceful, [3mThe Right to 
Private Property [0m(Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1988), C. B. Macpherson, [3mThe Political Theory of Possessive Individualism [0m(Clarendon 
Press, Oxford, 1962), the Introduction to Istvan Hont and Michael Ignatieff (eds.), [3mWealth and Virtue [0m(Cambridge University Press, 
1983), and Matthew Kramer, [3mJohn Locke and the Origins of Private Property [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1997). Political obligation 
is discussed by W. von Leyden, [3mHobbes and Locke [0m(Macmillan, London, 1981); compare J. Dunn, [3mPolitical Obligation in its Historical 
Context [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1980), chapter 3. On toleration see especially the essays by Dunn and Goldie in O. P. Grell, 
Jonathan Israel, and Nicholas Tyacke (eds.), [3mFrom Persecution to Toleration [0m(Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1991), and Alex Tuckness, 
โRethinking the Intolerant Lockeโ, [3mAmerican Journal of Political Science, [0m46, 2002. Two major recent systematic studies of his 
work as a whole from a historical point of view are Ian Harris, [3mThe Mind of John Locke [0mand John Marshall, [3mJohn Locke, Resistance, 
Religion and Responsibility [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1994). Both authors (alongside John Milton and Victor Nuovo) also have 
extremely valuable essays in M. A. Stewart (ed.), [3mEnglish Philosophy in the Age of Locke [0m(Clarendon Press, 2000). There are 
several helpful articles in J. W. Yolton (ed.), [3mJohn Locke. Problems and Perspectives [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1969); see 
especially Ashcraft and Aarsleff. The most penetrating discussion of the evolution of Lockeโs own political commitments is to be 
found in Ashcraftโs study, [3mRevolutionary Politics [0m(Princeton University Press, 1986), in a number of studies by Mark Goldie, 
notably, โJohn Locke and Anglican Royalismโ, [3mPolitical Studies, [0mMarch 1983, and in the Introduction to Laslettโs edition of the [3m
Two Treatises [0m. There is a thoughtful and politically alert analysis of Lockeโs understanding of the conditions for governmental 
legitimacy in Peter Josephsonโs, [3mThe Great Art of Government: Lockeโs Use of Consent [0m(University of Kansas Press, 2002): compare 
Kirstie McClure, [3mJudging Rights: Lockean Politics and the Limits of Consent [0m(Cornell University Press, 1996). For the pressing 
issue of how far Locke succeeded or failed in doing justice to the formidably different practical predicaments and interests of 
women, see especially Carole Pateman, [3mThe Sexual Contract [0m(Polity, 1988), and A. John Simmons, โThe Conjugal and the Political in 
Lockeโ, [3mLocke Studies, [0m1 (2001), responding to Ruth Sampleโs, โLocke on Political Authority and Conjugal Authorityโ, [3mThe Locke 
Newsletter, [0m31, 2000. 

The writings of the main target of the [3mTwo Treatises [0m, Sir Robert Filmer, are available in convenient modern editions by Peter 
Laslett (Basil Blackwell, Oxford, 1949) and Johann P. Somerville (Cambridge University Press, 1991). The distinctiveness of 
Filmerโs views is best brought out in James Daly, [3mSir Robert Filmer and English Political Thought [0m(University of Toronto Press, 
1979). The background to his thinking can be approached through Gordon J. Schochet, [3mPatriarchalism in Political Thought [0m(Basil 
Blackwell, Oxford, 1975). The relations between the political theory of Locke and his 18 [1mth century successors are discussed in J. 
Dunn, โThe Politics of Locke in England and America in the Eighteenth Centuryโ, [3mPolitical Obligation, [0mchapter 4, and โFrom Applied 
Theology to Social Analysis: The break between John Locke and the Scottish Enlightenmentโ, in Hont and Ignatieff (eds.), [3mWealth 
and Virtue, [0min Stephen Dworetzโs somewhat brash, [3mThe Unvarnished Doctrine [0m(Duke University Press, 1990), and in Michael Zuckertโs 
learned and intelligent [3mNatural Rights and the New Republicanism [0m(Princeton University Press, 1995). I have attempted to assess 
the varying longevity and weight of Lockeโs impact upon subsequent political thinking in โWhat is Living and What is Dead in 
Lockeโs Political Thoughtโ, in Dunn, [3mInterpreting Political Responsibility [0m(Polity, 1990), โThe Contemporary Political 
Significance of John Lockeโs Conception of Civil Societyโ, in Sudipta Kaviraj and Sunil Khilnani (eds.), [3mCivil Society: History 
and Possibilities [0m(Cambridge University Press, 2001), and โMeasuring Lockeโs Shadowโ, in [3mLockeโs Letter on Toleration and Two 
Treatises of Government [0m(ed. Ian Shapiro, Yale University Press, 2003). There are important modern studies on Lockeโs political 
theory in French (notably those of Jean-Fabien Spitz), German, Japanese, and Italian. An annual periodical, [3mThe Locke Newsletter [0m
(up to 2000), now [3mLocke Studies [0m, published by Roland Hall, Department of Philosophy, University of York, provides regular 
information on current research into Lockeโs life and thought. Its first issue was an invaluable bibliography, since republished 
in a fuller form as Roland Hall and Roger Woolhouse, [3mEighty Years of Locke Scholarship. A Bibliographical [0mGuide (Edinburgh 
University Press, 1983). 

Six works which illuminate the background to important aspects of Lockeโs writings are Michael Hunter, [3mScience and Society in 
Restoration England [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1981); Quentin Skinner, [3mThe Foundations of Modern Political Thought [0m(2 vols., 
Cambridge University Press, 1978); Richard Tuck, [3mNatural Rights Theories: Their Origins and Development [0m(Cambridge University 
Press, 1979); and [3mPhilosophy and Government 1572โ1651 [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1993); John W. Yolton, [3mJohn Locke and the Way 
of Ideas [0m(Clarendon Press, Oxford, 1956); Richard H. Popkin, [3mThe History [0mof [3mScepticism from Erasmus to Spinoza [0m(University of 
California Press, Berkeley, 1979).
